0307477479|5.0|This review originally appeared on my blog, which can be found on my site [...].<br /><br />I just recommended this book to one of my beautiful friends.  I've always been rather stunned by a particular kind of physical beauty.  In both men and women.  I'm not talking seduction.  I'm talking a more substantive, sexless, almost holy admiration for the aesthetics in the human production (how it's all put together).  I'm serious.  I've always been this way.  In college, the beautiful boys kept my attention (join the crowd, right?).  As a young adult, I just stared, going to the Met for beauty one day , checking out people on the street for beauty the next.  Despite my own average appearance, I'd marry \\"up,\\" if you know what I mean.<br /><br />So this friend.  I won't fully describe her, since she probably wouldn't like that.  But she's capable of walking into a room wearing the pointiest yellow high-heels you've ever seen and fishnet-stockings with a businessy skirt, button-down blouse, and maybe red lipstick.  If I wore it, people would whisper and point.  Someone would say, \\"What's up with that?\\"  When she wears it, you think, \\"Wow.  That is the most sophisticated, elegant, chic, bold, magical creature ever.\\"  Canary yellow today, fuchsia tomorrow.<br /><br />It's true.  I have a thing for beauty.<br /><br />But this is the real story:  It's not just a physical beauty thing, it's an age thing.<br /><br />Just as I've always been a beauty freak, I've been captivated--okay, obsessed--with aging.  At first, I couldn't wait. While friends still talk with longing about the joy and freedom of childhood--and how great it all was--I always wanted adult experience.  (I'm not sure what I imagined, really.)  Get on with the aging.  Obviously, I didn't get it.  Once the aging starts, it never stops.<br /><br />In college, even though I was happy only a fraction of the time, I was already nostalgic before actually graduating.  My college life was well-documented in photos, scrapbooks, journals; it was filled with intense friendships and wistful moments.  I lamented its passing--and when that life slipped away, I tried to hold on.  I'm still friends with many of the individuals who haunted that era, and I'm still blown away by our adulthood, our marriages and children and mortgages, our skin and hair texture.  As each new phase of life comes and goes, the acute awareness of time has grown, morphed into a full-blown affirmation of a running theme of Jennifer Egan's novel:  \\"Time's a goon.\\"<br /><br />Don't I know it?<br /><br />At first, I didn't like the title, A Visit from the Goon Squad.  I suspected, having no real clue about it or its author, that it wasn't quite up to my snobby literary standards.  (Don't hate me, Jennifer Egan.)  Too trendy, too contemporary, too hip.  But it came highly recommended by a friend who really reads, so . . . .<br /><br />Okay, I loved it.  The novel is about a bunch of interconnected characters, mostly involved in the punk rock industry, who--in one way or another--are shown in light of their pasts, which may involve punk rock in San Francisco, kleptomania in Naples, a lion attack in Africa, or snorting gold flakes, among other things.  There are numerous narratives focused on different characters (though this is a novel and reads like a novel--not like a short story cycle).  They work together to express, ultimately, the weight of time.  There's something especially unique here; Egan doesn't do the usual thing with time:  talk about how the past haunts you for your whole life (though this may be true and, yeah, it's there--doesn't it have to be?).  Rather, she just shows that time is passing and it has its profound effect on our lives.  We change.  We're connected to others with equally profound and passing lives.  Time is, indeed, a goon.  She isn't particularly hopeless at all.  The book is sobering, but sweet.  I wasn't left with a bitter awareness of sagging breasts and bald spots, nor was I reminded ad nauseum of how, as in Bambi (which I just saw again), seasons pass and new babies are born (though there are babies in this book).  There is some of that, but there's more:  it's a portrait of the changes that occur over a lifetime, a picture of changeability, malleability, shifts in our worlds that work together to define us.<br /><br />More profound things could be said about the phenomenon of time, though Egan's portrait is fully satisfying.  More could be said, but she didn't have to say it.  I wasn't super crazy about the \\"slide show\\" she includes in one of the narratives.  But here's my big praise.  Every time--every time--we left one character for another, I was sad to leave him or her.  With each narrative, I was hooked.  The writing was great.  Contemporary, but definitely literary.  Smart writing.  Hip writing.  (Hey, it reminded me of my own!  Tongue is in cheek, okay?)  I like the New York stuff, from the neighborhoods to the attitudes.  I was impressed with some of the emotional landscapes (she describes how one husband, increasingly disaffected by his wife, folded his desire for her in half, and then in half again, and then in half again, till there was nothing left).<br /><br />Alas, it's a good book.  Though it may sound pretty harsh and negative to indict time as a goon, I felt rather happy at the end.  Life changes, but sad things may become happy things, the childless may have children, the lost may get found.  Like the punk rock industry, the music keeps changing.  So, yeah, time is a goon, but there's still good music coming.

0375701427|5.0|This review originally appeared on my blog, which can be found on my site at [...].    Fifteen minutes for Philip Roth.  That's all I've got, even though I loved this book and spent a couple weeks with it.  Two caveats (like the word \\"literati,\\" I like to use \\"caveat\\" often):  this was my first Roth and I did no scholarly research whatsoever.    So, imagine my pride and joy when I arrived upon--all by myself!--the comparison between AMERICAN PASTORAL and F. Scott Fitzgerald's THE GREAT GATSBY!  Wow!  Look what I came up with!  This is the new GATSBY!      Others thought of this already, I quickly realized.    So be it.  Still.  This is a darker GATSBY.  It is an ugly GATSBY.  Unlike Fitzgerald, it's not breezy, musical, or colorful.  But it's intelligent and so impressive for its narrative depth.  I was completely absorbed in the glove-making process.  This, from a girl whose eyes glaze over at the sight of instruction manuals or the sound of computer talk.  Roth goes on and on about gloves because his characters are glove-manufacturers.  And yet it's breathtaking!    But that's not all this book is about.  It's about the same stuff as GATSBY--though, dare I say, with more complexity, reality, and philosophical depth.  (Hey, please know this:  I love THE GREAT GATSBY.)  This book is later, post-GATSBY.  We're talking post-WWII, nuclear threat, the Cold War, Red Scare, the Vietnam War--up till Watergate and the mainstreaming of porn in the form of DEEP THROAT.  The American Dream had opened up and shut down.  Gatsby, so white and preppie in Long Island, had now become Jews escaping Nazis and poor Irish seeking a Promised Land too.  And forget the Hamptons.  We're in Jersey.  That last, remarkable page of THE GREAT GATSBY is turned and  . . . .    AMERICAN PASTORAL picks up with its own protagonist, Swede Levov.  And its own Nick Carroway too, who tells the story from the sidelines:  Nathan Zuckerman (imagine my own ego bursting when I put this together, thinking I had made an original discovery!).      I, of course, don't ever really \\"review\\" books.  This one intrigued me completely in that it really explores the American psyche.  I can't find the passage, but there's a wonderful one on the loneliness within the heart of humans--the inability to truly find communion with another.  And then he does this--possibly unintentionally:  he spells out the secularization of America.  At the very end of the book, Roth writes a seemingly tangential conversation between Dawn (a former beauty queen--how perfect is that for a symbol of the American Dream?) and Mr. Levov (a nice Jewish man belonging to another era) in which they \\"negotiate,\\" if you will, the marriage between a Catholic and a Jew.  They discuss who gets what:  baptism, bar mitzvah, Christmas, Easter.  But the truth:  it doesn't really matter.      I found the conversation brilliant, and I found myself wondering if Roth knows how brilliant it is.  The American Pastoral disappears as meaning is lost.      One final note.  The end of the novel.  I'm not going to give it away because I really hope you read this book.  But it ends with an interesting scene with an Academic, an intellectual.  At first, I felt that disappointment that comes at the end of a lot of literature because I wanted something else.  But then I found it brilliant.  Feel free to tell me what you thought.      This book also served as an opportunity for me to get into it a bit with my Jewish uncle.  Our family is an American Pastoral in its own right; we've got Jews, Catholics, Atheists, and Protestants.  (Jews who escaped Nazis and Poor Irish!)  Perhaps others?  But my Jewish uncle gave me the opportunity to escape my major summer preoccupation of making peanut butter and jelly sandwiches and applying sunscreen to preschoolers.  We got into it over Roth and the portrayal of Jews.  We touched upon such intriguing topics as Woody Allen, Mel Gibson, and Michael Jackson.  Thank you, Uncle Saul, for the Roth banter.Last night, prior to finishing this book, I read a goofy article on James Franco (Jewish!).  I guess he's behind a new Museum of Non-Visible Art (MONA), in which there's no actual art--just descriptions of it.  James Franco is a post-PASTORAL Renaissance Man--filmmaker, writer, even an Academic--so his MONA is the appropriate finale to these fifteen minutes with Roth.  Franco may be the ultimate postmodern artist, actually.  There is no real art in this museum.    I think Roth saw it coming.

0316056863|4.0|This is originally posted on my blog, which can be found on my website:  [...]<br /><br />When I was driving the kids to school today, my five-year-old daughter--known for her wit--asked, \\"Mommy, can you do anything silly?\\"  Like what?  Stand on my head?  Wear a clown's nose?  What?  (See, I can't even think of anything silly.)<br /><br />My kids will tell you that I don't do silly.  Daddy does silly.  Every night, I read them a story and then we trek into their bedroom, and they literally look for the silly thing daddy has done.  Perhaps the stuffed animals are hiding under covers, behind curtains, on top of the doorways.  Perhaps the stick horses are rodeo-bound in their race for secret closet recesses I simply don't know about.  The girls shriek because daddy--not mommy--is a blast.<br /><br />I'm funny.  Wow, am I funny.  Silly, I am not.<br /><br />For some reason--and I don't fully understand it myself--my writing has become humorous.  (I should say, so my three to four fans know, that Love Slave--book number two--is the really funny one.)<br /><br />I wasn't always so hilarious.  Once, I was a bore.  And then it changed, oddly enough, when I almost died in a car accident, and then--bam!--I was funny.<br /><br />I'm still a bore in person, though.<br /><br />This is most clearly demonstrated in the first phone conversation I had with the editor of Love Slave, who's highly respected in publishing.  I mean, when other highly respected people found out I was working with him (I was the sole seedy person involved), there was a rush of literary enthusiasm.  When he called me on the phone for the first time, he probably expected a regular stand-up comedienne, and--alas--it was just me, trying to quickly swallow my peanut-butter-and-banana sandwich so I could articulate respectable things intelligently.<br /><br />I prefer to exist in print.  Sometimes, though, one is required to speak to others in person and, yeah, that sucks.  Thankfully, I can write a witty line or two.  You better believe that when that first phone conversation ended with my highly respected editor, I rattled off a charming, smarmy, gut-splitting e-mail about copyediting.  Commas and periods!  Stop Now!<br /><br />Four things inspired me to write about humor-writing.<br /><br />First, I just finished Tina Fey's book, Bossypants.<br /><br />Second, I recently read an essay by David Bouchier called \\"The Comic Point of View:  Putting Humor in Your Fiction,\\" in the Julie Checkoway edited anthology, Creating Fiction, and it occurred to me (not without some surprise) that I may actually be one of those comic writer-types.  A humorous writer?  A humor-writer?  I never thought of myself like that.  I still don't, but it interested me nonetheless.<br /><br />Third, I've been an in-and-out-of-the-closet fan of Judd Apatow ever since I saw The Forty-Year-Old Virgin.  I kinda feel weird about liking him so much, since his films can be really raunchy.  (Incidentally, if those of you who know me personally knew who my Apatow-film supplier was, you'd be pretty surprised indeed.)  But I'm going to admit it now:  I'm a fan of Judd Apatow, especially of his Superbad.  There.  I said it.<br /><br />Fourth, I think I recently shared on Facebook an Onion post that was apparently in bad taste, and it got me thinking about one of the purposes--just one--of humor.<br /><br />Let's begin by personally addressing Ms. Tina Fey:  Tina, this will be the best thing you've read about your book.<br /><br />(I like to say those kinds of things in order to set myself up for disappointment when Tina never reads this, when it fails to live up to the promise and thereby launches an inferiority complex, and when others realize I'm all talk and no action).  You hear me, Tina!<br /><br />But, yeah, I've only recently become the hit of the party.  Let's see if this translates well.  I wrote one of my favorite jokes--I write jokes just like Tina!--for the wedding toast of one of my best friends.  Iguess you need to know that my husband is named Tim, and I like to present him as my own personal straight man.  This is a little unfair because he's quite funny himself.  But unlike me, he's private.  AND I'M PUBLIC!<br /><br />So I was the Matron of Honor (Matron!  That's a riot!), and I knew the man who performed the ceremony well.  We'll call him Mad Max.  I said this in my toast: \\"Mad Max has said that Tim and I remind him of Brad Pitt and Angelina Jolie in the movie, Mr. and Mrs. Smith.  I'm still trying to figure this one out.  Mad Max, is that because of their amazing good looks, or because they were trying to kill each other?\\"<br /><br />Does this translate well, or not really?  Because if I had one particular criticism of Tina Fey's book, it was that her favorite script moments--which were no doubt hilarious when performed on \\"Saturday Night Live\\"--didn't come off that great in writing.  I know how that goes.  So, did my joke work or not?<br /><br />Okay, let's think about my four influences for this post.<br /><br />First, Tina.  I do love when people my age succeed.  There's that.  I tend to read one \\"humor\\" book a year.  I think the most recent ones include Stuff White People Like by Christian Lander and I Am America (And So Can You!) by Stephen Colbert.  This time, I picked up Fey because of these words on the back cover:  \\"Once in a generation a woman comes along who changes everything.  Tina Fey is not that woman, but she met that woman once and acted weird around her.\\"<br /><br />Oh, I love that.<br /><br />Here's another similarity that we have.  We both went on cruises for our honeymoons, and we both fondly remember the towels in the shape of an animal left on your cabin bed each night in lieu of a mint.  Maybe you got a mint too.  I don't remember.<br /><br />And another one!  We both have scars on our faces!  Tina, I have MULTIPLE scars!<br /><br />I think my favorite chapter--I'm gonna make a point eventually--was about photo shoots.  This ishow it opens:  \\"People sometimes ask me, `What's it like to do photo shoots for magazines?'  `Do you enjoy that kind of thing?'  Let me be completely honest here.  Publicity and press junkets are just part of the job.  Your work is what you really care about because your work is your craft and your craft is your art and photo shoots are THE FUNNEST.\\"  And then she details how she'd be taken care of by the stylist's assistant, who \\"will be a chic twenty-year-old Asian girl named Esther or Agnes or Lot's Wife.\\"<br /><br />Why does this crack me up?  I'm about to make a point, I think.<br /><br />It has to do with Judd Apatow and The Onion.  My Tendency To Like Things In Poor Taste.  Am I about to say that Tina Fey's humor is in poor taste?<br /><br />Nope.  What I found especially funny about this chapter and others in her book is that she hones in on something true to human nature.  In this case, it's this desire for attention, this maybe feminine objectification thing in which we-hate-that-sex-symbol-shite-but-want-you-to-want-me-so-we'll-contort-and-pay-homage-to-photoshop.  And let's not forget the authentic details:  pretty Asian girls with Special Names.  The combo is what does me in:  the truth and the detail.  It makes her disarmingly funny.  That is the same exact thing that got me with Salinger's Catcher in the Rye.<br /><br />What about the Asian girl?  Is that offensive?  It is not. But it could be if someone else uttered it.  Tina Fey is no racist, and it isn't even worth discussing.  I'm not advocating that whole thing where people get to say racist things if they're a member of that group or if they have it in good with that group.  I hate that stuff.  What I am advocating is this:  telling the truth is funny.<br /><br />Humor is, then, a truth-telling device.  This is so not original.  Sorry.  I'm thinking aloud, trying to figure out why I like that \\"Lot's Wife\\" business so much.<br /><br />Let me make a confession:  I was a little envious of Fey's ability to make fun of her physicality.  I think many of the people I personally find funny give up this kind of vanity--they make fun of their bodily imperfections.  Their bodies become tools.  And I'm just not there.  I don't like to draw attention to my physical appearance.  You don't believe me, do you?  I mean, yeah, I think I've talked about aging, breasts, et al.  In truth, I'd have a hard time putting myself up for physical scrutiny--and I envy the comedian/comedienne who does.<br /><br />Second, Am I A Humorist?  Is That What You Should Call Me?<br /><br />No, please don't.  I want to be a literary fiction-writer, okay?  That's what I want.  Sure, I'm funny.  Sure, Tina and I both went on cruises, but I'M A LITERARY FICTION-WRITER.  I WRITE LITERARY FICTION.  END OF STORY.  THAT'S IT.<br /><br />No one puts George Saunders in that box, do they?  You don't hear Lorrie Moore pigeonholed in this way.<br /><br />Yeah, I thought Me Talk Pretty One Day by David Sedaris was hilarious.  And, while we're here, I want to pay tribute to one-time Arizona resident, Laurie Notaro.  Laurie, a successful writer (Are you a humorist, Laurie?  What are you?) moved to Oregon, but she used to write a column in the Arizona Republic's weekly entertainment paper, \\"The Rep.\\"  (I hope I have my facts right.)  Laurie, you were the best writer there.  I will now admit that the protagonist of my forthcoming novel, Love Slave, is a columnist--and Laurie Notaro's very, very funny columns did influence me.  I loved her columns and, with others, mourned her departure.<br /><br />But what are you, Laurie?  What do you call yourself?<br /><br />Third, Judd.  I could go in many directions with this.  I could talk about the differences between an Apatow \\"sex farce\\" and others in the theaters--the redemptive ends, the centrality of something tender and sweet, etc.  But it's the Tina thing again:  honing in on something true to human nature and combining it with the authentic detail.<br /><br />I remember seeing that lovely, that perfect, that supreme moment when Steve Carrel, the Forty-year-old Virgin himself, races away on his bicycle while Asia's \\"Heat of the Moment\\" plays in the background.  That's a Lot's-Wife-Moment, if you ask me.  I can't explain it.  But do you see it?  Do you see how funny that is?  Is it me?<br /><br />I love a good Judd Apatow film.  And isn't the sex-stuff; it's the other stuff:  the true moments.  But note something special about Apatow:  his true moments are rendered in a sweet way.  Apatow's people are nice.  It's McLovin' in Superbad.  It's the talk of James Franco's \\"bubbe\\" in Pineapple Express.  It's the Dracula Puppet Opera in Forgetting Sarah Marshall.  These are glimpses into human nature.  I mean it!  I love this stuff!<br /><br />Fourth, my Onion offerings in poor taste.  I recently posted the following \\"news item\\" from The Onion:  \\"Nation's 10-Year-Old Boys: 'If You See Someone Raping Us, Please Call The Police.'\\"  This was, of course, in response to the Penn State child-molestation horrors.  I didn't get many \\"likes,\\" I gotta admit.  Not many folks wanting to laugh aloud about that one.<br /><br />But I will!  Yes, I will!<br /><br />Because I'm a believer.  I believe in Truth, Beauty, and Goodness.  I believe in irony, especially:  its power, its bite, its charm.  And I'm willing to risk poor taste.<br /><br />You know why?  Because that's how strongly I feel about the downright wickedness involved in the rape of children.  And it's not terribly rare for funny people to call us on our obliviousness, our blind eye, our polite deference to good taste.<br /><br />I sound a little incensed, don't I?  I offer a half-hearted apology.  I am a little sensitive about this.  I do draw the line at times.  Here's one little example.  Tim and I were in tears when we watched Borat.  We turned off Br√ºno.  Too gross.  That said, I'm skittish about quick dismissals of things in poor taste.<br /><br />I guess I should say somethingabout Russell Brand.  Why him?  Because of our recent Brand experience.  I can't really call myself a fan--not like I can with Apatow.  I do think Brand is over-the-top.  I feel pretty Protestant when I hear him.<br /><br />But, one lazy night, Tim and I watched a Russell Brand concert on TV.  (Tim, incidentally, is usually right there next to me on all this stuff; he may not agree with me--I won't speak for him this time--but he's present, okay?)  I'll be honest with you.  It was too much.  Too much sex.  A little yucky.  I kept wanting to make sure the kids were asleep.<br /><br />Though there was this:  He came off as smart (I know nothing about him whatsoever), and one of his jokes was so true, so bitterly true, such a moment of insight into the meaning in life, reflected in a very particular way about what it means to be human, that I felt--momentarily--slayed.<br /><br />I can't repeat the joke, though.  Because it was obscene.<br /><br />Now all I want to do is raise some questions:  Is that insight, that moment of enlightenment, worth the overall obscenity?  How far is too far?  When do you throw the baby out with the bathwater?  What does it mean to go too far?<br /><br />Humor is a freakin' weapon!<br /><br />You know how I like to think of it?  You know those crazy terrorists who use women and children as human shields?  Well, a humorist uses him- or herself in the battle.  They'll expose themselves, standing vulnerable, for the joke.  It may boil down to this:  What Is The Joke?<br /><br />\\"That Joke Isn't Funny Anymore\\" is a Smiths' song, by the way.  And, if this is a book review of Bossypants, perhaps I should offer a little harsh criticism--just a little (besides the translation of the live performance into the written word).  Tina, is this okay?  I mean, I feel comfortable being honest with you, because we're--you and me, You and I--the SAME.  Okay?<br /><br />Like Tina, I too have stood over the kitchen sink eating my kid's dinner till she demandedit back.  Like Tina, I definitely/absolutely/completely/utterly/all-those-words-meaning-\\"fully\\" had the breastfeeding issue presented to me much like the Problem of Good and Evil.  Like Tina, I'm a working mom.<br /><br />It was the Working Mom Thing that gave me pause.  It struck me as, um, well, mildly defensive?  A little guilt-ladened?  Tina, do you hate me?  I don't know--maybe it's me.  I do know that I'm a little hypersensitive when it comes to the topic--so it's not entirely impossible that I'm misinterpreting it. 0060582545|5.0|Matilda was, according to Roald Dahl, one the most difficult books that he ever wrote, but it is also one of his most popular, standing alongside Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, BFG and The Witches. It is a story that revolves around an exceptional little girl, whose intelligence is unrecognized by her parents. Because of this, she learns right from the start that she has to do everything by herself to get exactly what she wants. Very episodic in its first half (incidents with Matilda's parents), the story's plot shifts in a straight path in its last half as Matild enters school and has to face an extremely vicious Headmistress, both to the students and to the teachers.<br /><br />Those that have read Roald Dahl's books will know that children in his stories face difficult and cruel situations from imposing figures of authority. This will not please certain parents or adults who may consider those things too scary and upsetting for children; even accusing those books of promoting rebellious ideologies. But to me those opinions are just rubbish and untrue. Instead, I think this book is an excellent way to warn children and adults that there are adults who have no respect for them and that it is essential to stand up to them. In fact, every time I have read Roald Dahl's books, I have always felt that he is one of those writers who treats children, and readers, with respect because he is showing to them the brutal truth about the world, the adults and the bullies - including those that are respectable authority figures - who think that they can get and do whatever they want because they have the higher power.<br /><br />As for the audiobook version, I was amazed by Joely Richardson's reading. She has the perfect voice tone for each character and she makes the reading such a pleasure that I didn't need to have the book to follow the storyline, and I never got lost by what she said. 0439813786|4.0|Although \\"The Invention of Hugo Cabret\\" contains over 500 pages, it is deceptively short. I was able to finish the book in an afternoon. This has much to do with Brian Selznick's illustrations as well as his compelling story. In reality, the book is a graphic novel. Images are as important as words in telling the story. That seems fitting considering the prominent role that the movies play in the whole story.  The story focuses on Hugo Cabret a boy who is twice an orphan. First he loses his father tragically in a museum fire. He is then forced to live with his drunken uncle at a Paris train station in 1931. Hugo's life is difficult. He is mistreated until the uncle's untimely demise caused presumably by alcohol. Hugo desperate to connect with his dead father discovers another world in the person of Georges M√©li√®s.  At one point in the story, Hugo tells Isabelle, goddaughter of Georges M√©li√®s, \\"Did you ever notice that all machines are made for some reason...Maybe that's why a broken machine always makes me a little sad, because it isn't able to do what it was meant to do...Maybe it's the same with people...If you lose your purpose..it's like you're broken\\" (374). Hugo and Georges both are broken, and only by finding each other do they get \\"fixed.\\"  Brian Selznick weaves a story of clockwork, automata, fantasy, magic, and movies. I am actually a little surprised how well he does it. Today, we have a separate understanding of magicians and movie makers, but in the early days of film, the line was blurred. Selznick successfully creates this world and makes a fun book.  The only part that I found distracting in the book was the emphasis on deception and stealing. Characters are constantly hiding secrets and stealing from one another. No character seems entirely noble in this regard. At first, I wondered if the deception was part of the brokenness. It certainly gets in the way of the characters finding their purpose. However, Hugo recalling a picture of Prometheus that he saw at the Academie du Cinema Francais fondly describes the god as a thief. This is done without any irony or explanation. Moreover, movies and magic are both about deception. They both create the illusion of things that are not there. Still, there are distinctions between Prometheus and your average thief or a magician and a liar. I would have preferred that the author helped us see those distinctions.  That being said, the \\"Invention of Hugo Cabret\\" was a great book.

0670022950|4.0|Before the first world war a long period of relative peace, growing international trade and Englightenment science, art and cultural development led people to think major violence was obsolete. Oops. The point is that violence often comes in spasms. It takes a generation or two for pressures to build up and for people to forget or recover from past wars. I think the fundamental reason people fight is to get stuff they need or want. Now that we know that chimps fight genocidal wars for territory, we can surmise that violence between in-groups versus out-groups goes back millions of years, perhaps tens of millions of years. Fighting for territory, females or resources goes way back and is hard wired in pretty strongly. Takes strong institutions (international treaties, education, violent sports as surrogates, etc.) to keep peace. Hard to overestimate the key role of demography. I made a list of 28 countries that used to fight but are now peaceful (Germany, France, Japan, China, etc.)and another list of 28 countries where at least 100,000 people had died violently in recent decades (Iraq, Guatemala, Bosnia, etc.). Average fertility rate for the peaceful former \\"hereditary enemies\\" was 1.6, TFR for the violent countries was 4.7. I rest my case.<br /><br />It should be noted that framing responses to the 9/11 attacks as a \\"war on terror\\" condemns the U.S. to never ending violent conflict. Technology exists to kill historical or more than historical numbers of people, although, with 7 billion of us, it takes a lot of deaths to match historical percentages. If we can get through the next century of resources shortages that seem likely due to the collision of growing populations with declining resources (especially soil, climate and fossil fuels)Dr. Pinker's idea that violence is going out of fashion will get more credible. Right now feels more like a pause between collapses to me. But we do have the tools (birth control) to reduce demographic pressures and demonstrably on the evidence, undercut the fundamental drivers of violence.<br /><br />Dr. Pinker might want to check out a few videogames that seem to me to teach a culture where violence is a preferred means of conflict resolution.Or visit a rifle range where he might find Evangelicals teaching kids to shoot in preparation for Armageddon. Also gun sales statistics. We ain't out of the violent woods yet.ce, undercut the fundamental drivers of violence. B00000E2VC|5.0|I am a teenager going through high school who checked out this CD from the local library on a whim. I didn't expect much since I've never heard any of the pieces on this album, nor have I had any experience with Michelangeli. My friend, who knew a little bit of Chopin, asked to borrow it before I had the chance to listen. Since she quickly fell in love with this album, I decided to give it a try while studying. First impressions were positive - the pianist was good and not too showy and the music was typical Chopin. Little did I know that this would become one of my most treasured albums.<br /><br />It grew on me. This simple album of simple Chopin pieces somehow acquired well over a hundred full listens on iTunes. I would listen to it walking to the bus, while it was raining, while I am bored, whenever. I would recall fond memories when I hear Mazurka No. 47 or Scherzo No. 2. Mazurka No. 43 would make me feel sad while the next Mazurka would try its best to comfort me. I would hum the melody of Mazurka No. 45 for whole days. Never before has an album made such an emotional impact on me.<br /><br />What is the deal with this album, then? How could a bunch of simple Chopin pieces get to be so loved? It's simple - Michelangeli took these pieces under his wing and infused them all with a job - to make us FEEL thoroughly sad. After all - the most important things in our lives spur a strong emotional response - it can be our lover, or the sunset, or even a good old shower. Thus, these selection of Chopin pieces acted as a source of empty sadness.<br /><br />I simply cannot put into words the emotional power of this album. Maybe it's like waking up on a nice, perfect day but feeling supremely dominated by sadness. Maybe it's the feeling of true loneliness. Maybe it's a sense of nostalgia. What I can say objectively is give this album a listen. You won't regret it.

This time it is Steven Pressfield's story that accompanies me in my journey into the new year. His story is one of years of failure, of novels nearly completed  but thrown away, of living out of a van, of his screen play's critical and popular disasters. Yet Pressfield is the author of numerous international bestsellers including The Legend of Bagger Vance.  Book One entitled \\"Resistance : Defining the Enemy\\" explores the greatest stumbling block to the artist: Resistance. Resistance is not only the enemy, but it is also a stone cold killer whose only goal is keep you from doing what you were meant to do. Pressfield reveals the importance of resistance in our personal evolution. . Resistance, according to Pressfield is like a compass, pointing towards our self's true North. \\"The more important a call or action is to our soul's evolution, the more resistance we will feel. . .\\" (Pressfield, p. 12) In other words, the more you resist, the more you ought to be doing whatever it is you are resisting.  Procrastination and fear or near enemies of resistance. According to Pressfield, \\"Fear is an indicator. The more scared we are of a work or calling, the more sure we can be that we have to do it. It shows you what you have to do..\\" (Pressfield, p. 41)  Book Two is called \\"Combating Resistance: Turning Pro.\\" According to Pressfield the only way to overcome resistance is to do whatever it is you are afraid of or whatever you have been putting off. If you want to be a professional, he gives the following advice: 1. Show up everyday. 2.  Show up no matter what.  3.  Stay on the job all day.  4.  Stay committed over the long haul.  5.  Master your craft.  Equally important a a professional must expose her craft to the real world.  Pressfield didn't become a professional until his first screen play met with critical and commercial failure. His movie, King Kong Lives, was so bad that an employee at a theatre that was playing the film remarked to him, \\"Miss it, man. It sucks.\\"  Book Two further examines what it means to be a professional; Pressfield identifies several character traits that exemplify a pro: patience, persistence, humility, love and courage. All of these traits are necessary until the gods decide what to do with your work.  Book Three entitled \\"Beyond Resistance: Higher Realm\\" explains my last statement. According to Pressfield that the creative process belongs in the spiritual plane. He invokes the muses( daughters of Zeus) each day befores he begins his work.  He understands that he is not ultimately responsible for the art he creates. In invoking the miracle of creation he is merely an instrument of the great creator.  Pressfield's most compelling and perhaps controversial argument deals with the implications of an unlived life.  The question he asks is what happens when we let resistance persuade us not to persue a higher calling: \\"Could our unlived lives have exacted their vengence upon us in the form of cancer?\\" (Pressfield, p. 134-5) If we are blocked in our creative and spiritual lives, we are not who we are meant to be. \\"Our job in this lifetime is not to shape ourselves into some ideal we imagine we ought to be, but to find out who we are and become it. If we were born to paint, it is our job to become a painter.\\" (Pressfield, p. 146)

I will not be talking about the fact that Jared Leto is an actor.<br /><br />When I first heard that this was a concept album, and that it was produced by Bob Ezrin - I had to check it out.  30 SECONDS TO MARS is a powerful, concentrated blast of fresh art metal that tells a great tale of aliens invading planet Earth.  The music immediately reminds me of everything that influenced the band ... Nine Inch Nails, Tool... it's all here.  There is a decidedly industrial sound to this whole album - which lends to the concept-album legacy... every song is mixed identically.  The mix is full of presence and volume.  Everything is loud and normalized - not anything I expected from Ezrin.  This is an album you can just press play and leave on as background music... there are no weird orchestrated moments or any distracting voice-overs (well, except for that bit at the very end - which is thankfully tasteful.)<br /><br />Since I'm a prog junky, I need to take a little time to scrutinize the performances enclosed here:  The rhythm section is consistently tight and pounding... the drum parts are definitely one of the best things about the album - and one of the most prog elements of the album as a whole.  Leto's brother does a great job and plays with a lot of passion.  This is a GREAT drummer.  Not super-technical.. but consistently tight and almost machine-like - just listen to the last minute of \\"The Mission\\" and you'll be playing air drums before you know it.<br /><br />The guitar tone is brutal.  The tone of the guitars layered with the thick synths create a juicy wall of sound that lends a great tone to the entire album.  The playing isn't anything remarkable, but it serves a purpose.<br /><br />Leto's voice is really strong throughout the album.  This guy can hit some sick high notes (as further evident in the band's newer material).. a lot of vocal edits, but they're tastefully done. The vocal mix is a huge part of the band's futuristic sound on this album.<br /><br />In closing, this album will forever be a sleeper.  The new radio-pop direction (and success) of the band in 2011 really tosses this album into the bargain bin for life.  Any prog fan who might enjoy this album will be unlikely to ever hear it, while fans of the band's new sound mostly have written the album off as a mistake. I recommend to \\"view\\" this film from Disk2 as this version shows all the surviving original intertitle artwork.  Keep in mind that Murnau presented this film with various \\"tinting\\" schemes (to evoke particular moods perhaps) and so, staying true to the original, this restoration is \\"tinted\\".  This is perhaps the one thing I do not \\"like\\" about the film.  It is artistically glorious but it wreaks havoc with picture uniformity.  Fritz Lang did not tint Die Nibelungen (1924) and that film & restoration is sooo much cleaner, but uncle Friedrich wanted mood tints... oh well, c'est la vie I guess.  That said, let me be clear.  This is a great film, and a gift to the world of the 7th art from the Weimar republic.    Moreover, we should all bow low to the masters who did the restoration work.  They have done wonderfully, admirably, magically.  One can compare the details seen in the background (church, houses, other buildings, fountain, gateways, walkways) with what survives today.  It is miraculous that all these elements have survived the allied bombings of WW2, but they are still there.  The extras in Disk 1 guide the observant tourist in how to will pick these details up.  If you love classic cinema you'll appreciate this as well.

1416955070|1.0|I have not read Cassandra Clare's fan fiction, although I do know that she started in fanfic. You can tell. For a fanfic, I would consider this to be very good, but I expect more out of a published novel.  Clare's writing style is nothing to get excited about. It moves the story along, but does nothing to add to it. I give her props for not abusing fragments (a pet peeve of mine) as a lot of YA writers do these days. However, she writes like someone who has just started getting into creative writing--she has figured out how to string words together, but she hasn't figured out how to use them to tell a story. Instead, she tries to find out clever ways to describe something or pulls out a thesaurus where none is needed.  However, everything in this book--plot, characters, settings--is cliche. This book has been written many times before and better. Since you know how everything is going to play out from page one, there's really nothing to get excited for in City of Bones.  She goes out of her way to reference things in anime and fandom which I assume is supposed to be cute, but I find it obnoxious. In fanfic, that might be acceptable, but not in a published novel. She also distracts from the plot by having her characters have a lot of \\"witty\\" banter. For the most part, I didn't find it particularly witty or funny, and it's never a good sign when another character in the book has to remind the other characters (and you) that the plot is still happening. That's very sloppy writing.  I really did not care for the main characters. Jace, the Love Interest, is one of your typical \\"Bad Boy\\" types who we are repeatedly told is \\"charming.\\" Over and over, Clare will tell you that he is charming, oh so charming. To quote The Princess Bride, \\"You keep using that word. I do not think it means what you think it means.\\" Perhaps as a 14 year old I might have been fooled into believing Cassandra Clare that her character is, indeed, just so charming, but as an adult... His actions, hisattitude, and the way he talks do not make him a charming or attractive character. If I met Jace in real life, I would think he was a rude, obnoxious brat.  Then there's Clary. It is painfully obvious that Clary is a self-insert of the author herself. This is not inherently a horrible thing. I would be perfectly willing to overlook it if the book and character were better written. The trouble is, Clary is not a sympathetic lead. She comes off as being jealous and hateful. Important, plot-related things might be going on, but Clary can always find the time to be jealous of and \\"hate\\" someone for having nicer eyelashes than she does. There is only one other female character in the book, Isabelle, and of course the two of them don't get along because they're so jealous of the other's beauty (but of course Clary doesn't think of herself as beautiful) and because they have to compete for all the boys' attention (naturally). Like Jace, I have a feeling I would not like Clary Fray at all if I met her in real life.  The biggest thing that bothered me about City of Bones and the character of Clary was the constant bashing of people who aren't thin and/or conventionally attractive. Of course, all the main characters are thin and conventionally attractive--that goes without saying. But Clary spends an inordinate amount of time in the book picking apart her and other people's physical flaws (or more likely being jealous of their perfections and hating them for it). Then, of course, there's the lovely quote, \\"She wondered if there were any ugly vampires, or maybe any fat ones. Maybe they didn't make vampires out of ugly people. Or maybe ugly people just didn't want to live forever.\\" I realize Clare is going for humor, but that's still a very offensive line and definitely not the kind of thing I want to see in a YA novel. The implication is that people who are \\"fat\\" or not conventionally attractive have nothing to live for. Considering most readers of this book are likely to beyoung girls who may already have problems with their self-image, is that really the message they should be receiving?  Perhaps she's going for some kind of realism. Maybe there are a lot of teenage girls who spend a lot of time focused on the physical looks of themselves and those around them and are jealous of others and hate them for looking better than they do. I was never one of those girls, and I don't find that to be a sympathetic trait in anyone--fictional or not. Had Clare written the story as if she were aware of Clary's mental/emotional flaws rather than just her physical ones, that would have been acceptable. Instead, Clary is portrayed as the Every Girl and we are meant to sympathize and agree with her. Unfortunately, I don't.  Cassandra Clare is a capable writer, and she could even be a good writer. Unfortunately, in City of Bones, she is too self-indulgent, and too lacking in originality and self-awareness to pull off a decent novel. I have no desire to continue this series, but I would hope that her later works show improvement because thus far the talent is there, just not the ability.

1400052181|5.0|The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks explores the life of a woman whose cells are used in medical laboratories worldwide.  Henrietta Lacks, an African-American woman living in Georgia, discovered that she was terribly ill and sought the help of doctors. In the 1950s, when segregation was loud and proud, Henrietta received treatment from white doctors. While Henrietta slowly died, her doctors discovered that her cells were immortal. That is to say they needed no assistance in multiplying. Her cells could be multiplied and used repeatedly in various research efforts. Before and after Henrietta's death, doctors collected tissue samples from her, without her or her family's consent, and began using her cells in medical research. Doctors would eventually sell her cells for hundreds of dollars while her children and family lived in poverty. The total amount of money generated from sales of the HeLa cells is in the billions.<br /><br />Rebecca Skloot spent years researching and writing this text. Her dedication to telling Henrietta's story is evident, and readers will appreciate her commitment to presenting the facts in an accurate and bias-free manner. Many of the concepts discussed in the text are complex, but Skloot breaks down the issues so that individuals who have a loose grasp on science can understand. This book examines medical ethics and race and how the two intersect. It is a compelling read that will encourage readers to think about their medical rights and how little control we have over the tissue samples and blood taken during routine medical examinations. Skloot's debut novel is thoughtful and engaging.<br /><br />You should read this book, because it is a fascinating account of a mother who died tragically, but whose cells continue to thrive today. The text will help you understand how African Americans have been used in medical experiments for years. If you are interested in learning more about medical experimentation and bioethics, this is the book for you.

0385334176|4.0|Inaction is action. Like the symbolic neutron bomb that wipes out the inhabitants of Midland City leaving all the inanimate stuff intact, Rudy's attempt to make himself a \\"neuter\\" hurts those around him. For example his insistence on referring to himself as a \\"murderer\\" breaks up one of his brother's marriages. even so he does succeed in limiting his effect on the physical world as he builds no family and has no lovers. For him after the neutron bomb wipes everyone out, nothing has changed. I read this while struggling with my own guilt over a bad thing I did in my past, and while Vonnegut says point blank that Rudy's accidental shooting in his childhood of a pregnant woman is meant to stand for \\"all the bad things I have done\\", don't expect any feelgood message about forgiving yourself or letting the guilt go. The message I took from it is that causing others pain is inevitable for a living being, and since some things you do or don't do will have bad side effects, trying to do nothing - to be a neuter - will have bad consequences too, except it is even worse because by its nature that life won't lead to anything good; it's pure bad side effects.  Take Rudy's father's friendship with Hitler before he became the devil. \\"Only an hour before, Hitler had sold his overcoat so that he could get a little something to eat, even  though winter was coming on. So there is a chance that, if it weren't for my father, Hitler might have died of pneumonia or malnutrition in 1910 [...] My father could have strangled the worst monster of the century, or simply let him starve or freeze to death. But he became his bosom buddy instead. That is my principal objection to life, I think: it is too easy, when alive, to make perfectly horrible mistakes.\\" But if Rudy's father had strangled Hitler when he was a nobody, Rudy's father would have gone either to jail or, if he had tried to convince people that Hitler was going to become the next Hitler, to a nuthouse. To anyone who did not know Hitler's destiny - which would have been everyone - letting him freeze to death would seem a perfectly horrible mistake. 0380730405|4.0|Don't worry, my review is not going to give away any key plot points. In fact, I'm not even going to discuss the plot since the suspense of this novel rides on the story's unpredictable twists and turns. \\"Rebecca,\\" published in 1938, is a Gothic thriller in the tradition of \\"The Mysteries of Udolpho\\" and \\"Jane Eyre.\\" All of the elements required for a creepy Gothic romance are here: 1) a Byronic hero haunted by his past who may or may not be a villain; 2) a beautiful dead woman, the novel's titular character; 3) a mysterious stone mansion overlooking the sea; 4) a village idiot, who appears to know more about Rebecca's death than he lets on; 5) a scary housekeeper; and 5) a high-strung, impressionable narrator given to reverie and fits of bravery who periodically is seized with the urge to explore the mansion's uninhabited west wing.<br /><br />Daphne DuMaurier has a special gift for creating ambience. In \\"Rebecca,\\" Manderley, the ancient estate in Southern England where most of the novel's action takes place, becomes a character unto itself. Manderley, with its high-vaulted ceilings and stone terraces, its lawns extending down to the sea, its woods, its coves, its gardens, is at once welcoming and forbidding. DuMaurier uses botanical imagery to convey these contradictory qualities: \\"blood-red\\" rhododendrons, impressive yet ominous, form a wall fifty feet high at the entrance to the house; in the wood, which is carpeted with bluebells, beauty commingles with horror as the flower-filled \\"Happy Valley\\" abruptly gives way to the sea and the cove where Rebecca died; beech trees \\"with white, naked limbs\\" come together like an arch over the serpentine driveway leading to house, creating a claustrophobic atmosphere. Throughout the book, flower imagery is used to sustain tension; the character of Rebecca is associated with the intoxicating fragrance of Manderley's azaleas.<br /><br />DuMaurier is quite skilled at keeping the reader in doubt as to which characters are the good guys and which characters are the bad guys. Is ghoulish housekeeper as evil as she seems? Is the brooding owner of Manderley to be trusted? Is the narrator, whose name is never mentioned, a reliable source? And what was Rebecca __really__ like? This is a great novel and a fast read. I highly recommend it. There's a reason why it's considered a classic.od guys and which characters are the bad guys. Is ghoulish housekeeper as evil as she seems? Is the brooding owner of Manderley to be trusted? Is the narrator, whose name is never mentioned, a reliable source? And what was Rebecca __really__ like? This is a great novel and a fast read. I highly recommend it. There's a reason why it's considered a classic.

0143038419|5.0|(Audio version) As a depth psychotherapist, I found this book captivating and the author's experiences absolutely authentic. I applaud Ms Gilbert's bravery in baring her soul and exposing her conflicts to the world. We all want to get over our hang-ups, find our true selves, and live our lives to the fullest, but few will do the soul-searching necessary to accomplish it. When in anguish, Ms Gilbert uses her journal to dialogue with her inner voice, and finds solace. Some call this inner voice God. Jungians call it the Self. Jung developed the method of inner dialogue, but it is an ancient and valid practice. In her journey, Gilbert opens herself to her scariest and most shameful (to her) parts, and not only examines them, but welcomes and accepts them. This is the essence of analytical work. One can accomplish it in the consulting room, in an ashram, or anywhere one can find a psychologically safe space in which to do it. It does not require travel or money. Readers who are disappointed at not being presented with the secrets of the universe miss a subtle point. Each person's experiences are different. We tend to forget that once we have experienced an enlightened state, we must bring our insights into the world and live them concretely. If we stay in our heads we have learned nothing. Through self-examination and inner dialogue, Gilbert puts her previously complex-ridden ego on the back burner so her behavior is no longer dictated by it. Her actions in the end of the story are clearly those of an integrated person who has learned to consult her whole self in making choices.
B002MQYOFW|5.0|From a more technical, literary standpoint, The Hunger Games is written in an extremely simple fashion. It isn't particularly eloquent or overly descriptive. However, its tone is perfectly suited for a young adult reader. Set in the (very dark) future of North America, the general plot surrounds a suppressed and demoralized populace smothered by a spiteful, totalitarian government. In this world, starvation is common and the death of children by means of forced gladiator-style combat is the highest form of entertainment. The characters in the story feel beautifully flawed and carry a level of striking realism that contrasts wonderfully with their far-fetched, dystopic surroundings. The protagonist, Katniss, is faced with unbelievable hardship, violence, and impossible choices, which engages even the most demanding and seasoned adult reader. This book is a must-read for all lovers of adventure, suspense, tragedy, and justice.

1591843790|3.0|\\"They are joined one to another,<br />They stick together and cannot be parted.\\" -- Job 41:17 (NKJV)<br /><br />Enchantment is Guy Kawasaki's extension of Robert Cialdini's classic, Influence, in trying to integrate lessons from behavioral psychology to the level of creating a marketing program that makes enough of a lasting impression with a superior offering to make people change their habits. The book is mostly based on summarizing research done by others, with examples drawn either from the research methods or Mr. Kawasaki's personal experiences as a consumer. For someone who wants to understand how to be more influential in encouraging engagement and stickiness, the book is a decent summary that will save reading a lot of other sources and assembling them into a program. However, the book doesn't add much beyond being a digest of that research.<br /><br />I mainly disagree that the book lays out a program for creating \\"enchantment,\\" a psychological state that captures how people behave when they are doing something they love, just for the joy of it . . . such as finger painting with a child and not caring about the mess while having a giggling good time. Now a book with a title like \\"Enchantment\\" is going to sell a lot more copies than one about \\"Engagement and Stickiness\\" so I don't blame him for using it . . . but I think he's overselling his contents.<br /><br />I appreciate that Mr. Kawasaki clearly states that he is on the side of ethical \\"enchantment.\\" I found that the advice didn't always seem to match up with that standard. One glaring example is calculating how much swearing to do and when to make the best possible impression on listeners. To me, that seems more manipulative than enchanting.<br /><br />A lot of behavior is also defined so narrowly that it will create an impression, but one that may well be forced . . . rather than free flowing. Smiling is a good example. Study the smiling countenance of some celebrity you like. Chances are you'll feel that you are in the same room with the person, and that you are being looked upon as if you were the celebrity's favorite person in the world. That can be enchanting for brief periods of time until you realize that the celebrity is really just putting on a practiced smile for a camera. It's an act.<br /><br />Personally, I have been much more impressed in my business career by people who expressed actual interest in talking to me, acted with the absolutely highest integrity in every little thing, and honestly told me their innermost thoughts about what was good and not-so-good about their offerings. I didn't quite get that sense here. It was more like being prepared to put lipstick on a pig and present the pig as the greatest thing ever.<br /><br />I think the lessons and the storytelling would have gone a lot better if they had been applied to a recent launch of an offering that Mr. Kawasaki helped with. Then, the practical problems would have been more obvious, and some good advice could have been presented about how to apply the research. But perhaps Mr. Kawasaki is more of a speaker than a marketing consultant. It was hard to tell from reading the book.<br /><br />There's a quiz at the end that emphasizes remembering factoids from the book, rather than how to integrate the pieces together. That's emblematic of the book's overall limitations: It's more a pile of pieces than an integrated whole. It's easy to put the pieces of so many little rules together in a way that creates a Frankenstein monster clumping along looking for love rather than an enchanting engagement.<br /><br />One particular weakness is that the book is probably an attempt to describe now to work with the early triers in a new offering category. These people are intrigued by anything new that offers advantages. Geoffrey Moore has written well on the subject. But most offerings must shift their appeal to meat-and-potatoes advantages if they are to reach a mass market. All of that is mixed up together as though all customers were the same. That's always a mistake.<br /><br />Read, remember, and apply very cautiously.member, and apply very cautiously.

0670022950|5.0|This book is a page-turner.  Graphs, statistics and scholarly testings of a thesis don't normally appeal to me, with my little science and less math and my preference for fiction, essays and memoirs over tomes with big ideas. However, I'm a sucker for serious writing when it's executed with a human touch.<br /><br />This is certainly a serious book, stuffed with a big thesis and mountains of research.  It manages nevertheless to seem light on its feet and sometimes even whimsical: it's surprising how often the lyrics of Tom Lehrer turn up here, sometimes in the company of the thoughts of Hobbes and Hitler.  Even Pinker's personal experience gets into the act - he tells us of an inhumane lab experiment he once participated in, feeling it was wrong at the time but doing it anyhow.  He analyzes the reasons for his actions in the chapter on \\"Inner Demons\\".  The mundane is never very far from the horrific in that chapter.  It's where he describes the \\"Abilene Paradox\\", so named for a family trip made one afternoon in the summer of 1957 from one dreary Texas town to another.  Individually no member of the family wanted to make the trip: each was under the mistaken impression that all the others wanted to drive those dusty miles, eat that wretched dinner, see those unexciting sights and drive back home, everyone sweating like pigs before air conditioning had arrived in automobiles. (They each made the admission after the event, and one of their number went on to become a scholar and write it up.) Now that's the kind of experience we can all understand, and it may go some way to explaining why any of us might have been capable of torturing a rat in a lab in the 1970's or of carrying out orders in a concentration camp in the 1940's.<br /><br />We also learn from Pinker that though as a kid growing up in Montreal he was not much good at hockey or any other sport, he discovered a work-around for this deficiency: he found out early on that he had the ability to endure pain without too much complaint, and this was a capability he shamelessly exploited to get respect from the jocks who would otherwise have had no use for him. Such an observation goes far to explain the sort of cheerfulness that keeps breaking through the gloom of Pinker's depiction of the history of our species. We unprofessional and unscholarly readers can understand a thing like that, seldom as it is that we find ourselves in the readerly company of an engaging human being who happens also to be a first-rate thinker.<br /><br />I have to admit feeling another source of pleasure - perhaps a guilty pleasure - as I slowly read my way through this long book: moral uplift.  Could it actually be the case that our species is moving in the right direction?  That we are getting smarter, more imaginative and, yes, even more moral with every passing day? What serious writer since the days of the late Victorians has dared to say such things?  And make them seem plausible and worthy of intellectual respect to boot?<br /><br />I reckon it will be another century or two that the jury remains out on this one.  Will someone then dust off and read \\"The Better Angels of Our Nature\\" and find there a grand synthesis of all that was going right in the world at the beginning of our century but which remained unacknlowedged until Steven Pinker spoke out?  Or rather will the book be seen as a lonely and discredited exercise in middle-brow wishful thinking indulged in by a charming if utterly mistaken member of our inherently violent species?  I know where my heart wants to go on this one, and I am grateful for the book for having given my head some reasons for thinking my heart could be right.laint, and this was a capability he shamelessly exploited to get respect from the jocks who would otherwise have had no use for him. B006BQI1V2|5.0|SARAH'S KEY, novel:Tatiana de Rosnay, film:Gilles Paquel-Brenner<br /><br />The best plot summary of this film are words taken from a starred review by Publishers Weekly of the novel appearing on the Metacritic internet site:  \\"Julia Jarmond, an American journalist married to a Frenchman, is commissioned to write an article about the notorious Vel d'Hiv round up, which took place in Paris in 1942. She stumbles upon a family secret which will link her forever to the destiny of a young Jewish girl, Sarah. Julia learns that the apartment she and her husband Bertrand plan to move into was acquired by Bertrand's family when its Jewish occupants were dispossessed and deported... She resolves to find out what happened to the former occupants: Wladyslaw and Rywka Starzynski, parents of 10-year-old Sarah and four-year-old Michel. The more Julia discovers - especially about Sarah, the only member of the Starzynski family to survive - the more she uncovers about Bertrand's family, about France and, finally, herself.\\"<br /><br />This is a French film that stars the acclaimed actress Kristin Scott Thomas as the journalist, a wonderful and versatile M&eacute;lusine Mayance as the young Sarah, Niels Arestrup as the farmer who hides Sarah from the Germans, Fr&eacute;d&eacute;ric Pierrot as Jarmond's husband, and American actor Aiden Quinn in a small but stellar role in the last third of the film.  Although it is a Holocaust movie, there are very few graphic scenes and several glimpses of happier days: young Sarah playing on the bed with her brother in the Marais apartment; Sarah and another girl ripping the Star of David from their clothing, frolicking in a field of wheat, and bathing in a muddy river after their escape from a detainee camp; the two young girls sleeping in a farmer's barn with his guard dog.<br /><br />The true star (or villain) of the film is the Velodrome d'Hiver, an indoor bicyle track and sports stadium which was located in Paris near the Eiffel Tower. Built for the World's Fair in 1900, the small track was enlarged and moved several blocks farther away from the Tower to house events for the 1924 Summer Olympics. Damaged by fire in 1959 and demolished, the glass-topped structure was reconstructed for the movie by its crew. A bonus feature of the DVD shows the amazing reconstruction.<br /><br />Of the 28,000 Jews deported from Paris and Northern France by French police at the request of German occupiers, 13,152 men, women and children were placed in the Vel d'Hiv in July of 1942 for eight days. No toilet access and little food or water was available...and the comparison to the New Orleans Superdome catastrophe is drawn early in the film. (The film's modern-day action is updated to 2009 to allow this comparison.)<br /><br />This DVD is the film with English subtitles that had a limited USA release in July of 2011 to be touted as an Oscar 2012 contender. I had hesistated to rent a DVD with subtitles...but about a third of the film (the modern-day events) is in English...and the transition is smooth and not disruptive. From other Amazon reviews, it appears that the film closely follows the novel. Tatiana de Rosnay collaborated with Director Paquel-Brenner who wrote the screenplay.<br /><br />SARAH'S KEY is one of the best films I saw in 2011.<br />Highly recommended.<br />Janein 1900, the small track was enlarged and moved several blocks farther away from the Tower to house events for the 1924 Summer Olympics. B005K0HDGE|4.0|I have to admit it: if this book had been written by someone *other* than Stephen King, I would have rated it at five stars.  I do judge Mr. King more harshly than others because when something is written by America's storyteller, I expect more.<br /><br />It isn't that the plot was poor, or that the story was badly written.  It's that 11/22/63 didn't have the punch I've come to expect from a really great Stephen King novel.  Although there were some wonderful Stephen King-esque moments (such as a grisly murder, a bizarre individual known only as the 'Yellow Card Man', and the idea that the past has teeth), the plot dragged.  At times, it was as if I was living the story in real time.  The book would have been twice as good if it had been half as long.<br /><br />A problem with with writers who love their characters as Mr. King does is that they want to keep them onstage as much as possible.  Unfortunately, this can become like watching a friend's vacation photos: the friend may love their 200 pictures of Old Faithful, but after five pictures, everyone else is bored silly.<br /><br />Despite this, the story is engaging for the most part, and I did read it fairly quickly.  But it wasn't the gripping novel I'd hoped it would be.

0938045768|5.0|First, I want to say that this is a fantastic book.  Other reviewers have given good outlines of what is in the book, so I want to address the most common criticisms I have read.  Criticism 1:. Paul Wade is not a real person.  This started mainly because Paul Wade has shunned publicity, and because the book contains words and phrases more common to a native Brit than an American ex-con.  In fact, the book was edited with the assistance of Brits--including Wade's brother-in-law--which explains the words and phrases.  And several people who are personally acquainted with Wade have spoken out to confirm that he is, in fact, a real person (his friend Drew Reissman among them.)   Criticism 2: The book is overpriced. This is a common complaint of Dragon Door books, but \\"overpriced\\" is in the eye of the beholder.  Any book that you find disappointing will be \\"overpriced,\\" but will be completely worth it if you find the information of value.  Look at it this way: Instead of joining a gym, try CC to all final steps.  If you don't have the strength and development you want, then you can join the gym and be far ahead of the curve. In this case, the book would be a bargain since you will have saved months or years of gym fees.  Criticism 3: The book is too long.  I can understand that some people are only interested in the exercises themselves, and don't need to have justifications for why the author advocates the exercises. Not everyone will enjoy reading anecdotes about prison training but, personally, I found the anecdotes and stories very compelling.  The bottom line is that maybe the book is not as short as you like, but there is nothing included that is superfluous to the book's purpose.  You'll either like it or you won't.  Criticism 4: The title romanticizes prison.  The title might be sensational, but there is no glorification of prison life in the book itself.    Criticism 5: The book disses free weight and machine training.  This is true, but Wade gives meticulous justifications for why he thinks calisthenics are better for you.  I doubt his comments will put a stop to free weight training, but people should be aware of the consequences.  Look at it this way: taking ballet will likely mangle your toes and give you bad knees and ankles, but this hasn't stopped ballet dancing, nor is it likely to.  Just make informed choices.  Criticism 6: You can find the same information for free on the internet. Actually, you can find the exercises themselves, but not the progressive steps, which is the heart of the book and what makes it different from other material. I find myself constantly referring back to the the book for hints on form and ideas for getting the most out of each step.

B002VKE1K2|5.0|First of all, Happy New Year everybody!  I got Paranormal Activity for Christmas and watched it shortly after the new year began, late at night.  I see this film is one of those \\"love it or hate it\\" type horror flicks.  Actually according to the majority of reviews here on amazon, most believe it's a terrible attempt at the horror genre.  I couldn't disagree more.  Alright, for the first 20 minutes, I was definitely leaning towards \\"This is a stinky, overrated hunk of disappointment\\" just like the Blair Witch Project.  This is because we see nothing but a man and his girlfriend/wife walking around through their home doing ordinary things that all couples do.  However as the storyline got underway, I began to see what makes Paranormal Activity such a rewarding experience.  Let me state exactly what I think this movie does brilliantly- I've watched countless ghost documentaries in my time (just like I'm sure many of you have) and I believe Paranormal Activity is one of the very few that accurately illustrates exactly what the claims describe in those documentaries.  In other words, this is EXACTLY what a horror movie should be like- it captures the atmosphere and suspense of a real story.  Not that THIS particular story is based on true events mind you- but it sure reminds me of several stories I've heard from those documentaries.  Now, we can argue that many of those ghost documentaries could very well we totally fictitious or far-fetched, however let's say most of them are true, and that most ghost stories we watch on television ARE based on factual evidence.  With THAT in mind -and the frightening thoughts that run through my mind while watching any given ghost documentary- Paranormal Activity delivers exactly how I'd want it to.    A HUGE difference between the Blair Witch Project and Paranormal Activity is that nothing EVER happens in the Blair Witch Project.  It's just a bunch of young kids walking through the woods with a home video camera recording a dreary and typical walk through such areas.    With Paranormal Activity, there's REAL suspense that actually leads to something meaningful.  You'll know when such suspense arrives whenever the characters (a man and his girlfriend or wife) go to sleep and have a video camera set up in the corner of their bedroom to record the entire nights worth of events.  The next day the man reviews the footage.    Gradually, with each night that passes, the activity gets stronger and stronger, until... you'll be gasping in anticipation wondering what's going to happen next because it just gets REALLY crazy.  I'm not scared easily, but when a film sets out to replicate the kind of stories we've been hearing for years from all those ghost documentaries, yeah, this is pretty original stuff as far as horror storytelling is concerned, so I thoroughly enjoy Paranormal Activity.    The only thing that bugged me is that the film took place in San Diego.  I love San Diego, I have family there, the people I've communicated with have been super polite out there but... I just think a setting on the east coast given the high amounts of ghost activity reported over the years would have been a better location.    I highly recommend it.  Just don't judge the movie by the first 20 minutes which is a pretty boring amount of nothingness.  Keep watching and you'll see what makes the film so frightening.

1241681244|5.0|New York City in the Big '80s is the backdrop, lead actor, and punchline of this fantastic, sprawling, richly detailed and realized debut novel by famous New Journalist Tom Wolfe.<br /><br />\\"Punchline,\\" you might ask? Well, consider that the book was published just before a bond seller named Michael Milken galvanized the city with his sordid and high-profile arrest and just before a New York State schoolgirl named Tawana Brawley dropped a racially-explosive bombshell regarding her apparent victimhood at the hands of whites. In the book the bond salesman is named Sherman McCoy; the \\"victimized\\" student is named Henry Lamb. You kind of get the feeling Wolfe here was trying out something called \\"New New Journalism\\", i. e. reporting on the story before it even happens.<br /><br />Beyond its headline-stealing, the book is a rich stew of dramatic juxtaposition, political and social commentary, characterization and caricature, all boiled in the broth of a New York Wolfe toiled in for years and had researched at length for this novel. At times it reminds you of that famous New York-based TV series that came after, \\"Law & Order,\\" with its in-depth examination of what goes on in a Bronx courthouse as a DA and his minions examine the Lamb case and what to do with it. At other times it reminds you of that other '90s TV series set in the city, \\"Seinfeld,\\" with its amusement at people's baser selves and its development of an insular lingo. As he strides among Manhattan's upper-crust set, McCoy sees women as either older \\"social x-rays\\" or younger \\"lemon tarts;\\" partiers ask for sparkling water, not Perrier; and interior designers shudder at the memory of mirror overuse, a. k. a. \\"Mirror Indigestion.\\"<br /><br />Most famously, Sherman styles himself a \\"Master of the Universe,\\" the phrase that stuck with people most here. Sherman's hubris is the source of much of \\"Bonfire's\\" first-half satire; his downfall and discovery of a certain dignity provides \\"Bonfire's\\" second-half uplift.<br /><br />\\"Bonfire\\" doesn't focus solely on McCoy. There's a prosecutor named Kramer who shares his boss's longing for finding someone known as the \\"Great White Defendant,\\" a big-name criminal suspect who isn't black and poor. There's also Peter Fallow, an alcoholic reporter who finds his feet covering the Lamb case for the sensationalist New York Post, em, I mean City Light.<br /><br />Floating around the edges of the story are other characters whose minds we never get to enter. One, Reverend Bacon, is a black minister and pressure-group leader who steals money from a white upper-crust church and waves off their complaints, telling them he's their \\"prudent broker...who can control the steam\\" of the inner city. He's the one who works up the people when McCoy's case comes to trial. Bacon's resemblance to one Al Sharpton may or may not be incidental: Sharpton was another real-life character who didn't raise his profile to a national level until after \\"Bonfire's\\" publication. Wolfe isn't afraid to push racial buttons, or skewer targets of varied ethnicities, to make his points about the baseline dysfunction dogging NYC.<br /><br />The book doesn't always work at the same high level. Kramer starts out a pretty interesting character before devolves into a one-dimensional horndog, while Fallow's comic inability to grasp basic American culture seems strained. There's a speech in the middle of the book by a novelist dying of AIDS who references Edgar Allen Poe's \\"Masque Of The Red Death\\" (Poe Towers is also the name of the housing project where Lamb is from) in a way that feels like Wolfe straining for the kind of literary affect he captures elsewhere in stride.<br /><br />The book scores so often you ignore the off-notes like you would a weak pun in Shakespeare. In McCoy, Wolfe creates a wonderfully perverse yet strangely sympathetic figure. You will hate him in the beginning, yet as he is pulled and twisted by the system that fed him for so long, you begin to care, andrecognize in him something of a common humanity. I think it's this element that gives the book a hidden depth that stays with you after the laughs fade.<br /><br />For me, \\"Bonfire\\" is a richly rewarding reading experience, married to its time in a wonderfully redolent way yet not limited by it in terms of message. It's not like the kind of greed McCoy embodied went away with Ronald Reagan, Culture Club, and big hair, or that color-blindness now reigns supreme. I think mirrors may even be making a comeback. \\"Bonfire\\" is not only a laugh-getter and a reader roller-coaster; its social commentary remains sharp as ever.recognize in him something of a common humanity. The audio is amazing.  You can hear so much more instrumentation with the sound cleaned up and the vocals/harmonies sound spectacular.  My only gripe with Disc 1 is the obvious \\"remix\\" of the original version of \\"Silver Springs.\\"  I own the 4-CD Boxed Set, 'The Chain,' from 1992 which includes the original mix of the song, which was left off the album in 1977 and relegated to b-side status of the album's first single, \\"Go Your Own Way.\\"  I would have included the original mix of the song on Disc 1, and placed this \\"remix\\" on the second disc. A finished version of the song, along with additional lyrics, shows up on Stevie's 2001 album 'Trouble in Shangri-La,' and the song was a modest hit for her that year.  There was even a #1 dance remix to it.  The packaging to the album is great and includes some awesome photos that I think were included on the original vinyl.  The liner notes are insightful.  The casual Mac fans will not think the product is worth the price, but a true Mac fan will want this.
